ุงุณุชูุงูุชูุง ููุง ูู ุงููุงุญูุฉ ุงูููุฏูุฉ. 

[1mุญูุงุฉ ุดูุณุจูุฑ [0m

The standard life of Shakespeare is still Samuel Schoenbaum, [3mShakespeare: A Documentary Life [0m(1975; thereโs also a [3mCompact 
Documentary Life [0m, 1987): Schoenbaum gives the documentary evidence and assesses difficult questions with even-handed restraint. 
His [3mShakespeareโs Lives [0m(1991; paperback 1993) is a perfect supplement, taking as its subject the history of Shakespearean 
biography, and enjoying many of the more eccentric interpretations of Shakespeareโs life. Other recommended biographical works 
include James Shapiro on Shakespeareโs most productive year, [3m1599: A Year in the Life of William Shakespeare [0m(2005; paperback 
2006), Stephen Greenblattโs [3mWill in the World: How Shakespeare Became Shakespeare [0m(2004; paperback 2005), and Michael Woodโs book 
accompanying his television series [3mIn Search of Shakespeare [0m(2003). We quote often from the detailed work of our colleague 
Katherine Duncan-Jones: her biography of a less than likeable Shakespeare is [3mShakespeare: An Ungentle Life [0m(2010 โ a revised 
edition of her 2001 [3mUngentle Shakespeare [0m), and her account of Shakespeareโs immediate reputation is [3mShakespeare: Upstart Crow to 
Sweet Swan, 1592โ1623 [0m(2011). Park Honanโs [3mShakespeare: A Life [0m(2000) is especially good on the early years in Stratford; Jonathan 
Bateโs [3mSoul of the Age: The Life, Mind and World of William Shakespeare [0m(2008; paperback 2009) looks at Shakespeare and his 
context through the life-stages identified by Jaques in [3mAs You Like It [0m(โAll the worldโs a stage, / And all the men and women 
merely playersโ; 2.7.139-40). Charles Nichollโs in-depth analysis of a court case in which Shakespeare was called as a witness (a 
somewhat evasive one, it has to be said) is in [3mThe Lodger: Shakespeare on Silver Street [0m(2008). Lois Potterโs [3mThe Life of William 
Shakespeare: A Critical Biography [0m(2012) is not just (just!) a biography of Shakespeare: it is a biography of his theater world, 
informed by Potterโs unrivaled theatrical understanding. 

[1mุดูุณุจูุฑ ูู ุนููู [0m

The conditions of writing and printing drama are well covered by the contributors to David Kastan (ed.), [3mA Companion to 
Shakespeare [0m(1999), and Kastanโs [3mShakespeare and the Book [0m(2001) is a readable account of changes in editing and bibliography and 
why they matter. The British Libraryโs digital quartos website (http://www.bl.uk/treasures/shakespeare/homepage.html) allows 
access to all the early printed editions of Shakespeare: you can view a number of digital facsimiles of the First Folio online via 
the Folger Shakespeare Library (www.folger.edu). Lukas Erneโs controversial [3mShakespeare as Literary Dramatist [0m(2007) has turned 
old notions of the relation between long and short versions of Shakespeareโs plays on their head; he posits a Shakespeare who [3mwas [0m
interested in the publication of his plays. 

John Jowettโs [3mShakespeare and Text [0m(2007) is accessible and learned; his editions of [3mTimon of Athens [0m(2004) and [3mThomas More [0m(2011) 
extend the discussion of collaborative working practices. Andrew Gurrโs [3mThe Shakespeare Company, 1594โ1642 [0m(2004) studies 
Shakespeareโs works from the point of view of the structure and methods of the Chamberlainโs, later Kingโs, Men. Tiffany Sternโs [3m
Documents of Performance in Early Modern England [0m(2009) is one of those books that changes totally how you think about the early 
modern play โ she shows it not to be a unified text as published by Arden or Worldโs Classics, but rather an assemblage of 
fragments: songs, letters as props, parts, epilogues, prologues. David Crystal is [3mthe [0mexpert on Shakespeare and language, in a 
vast array of works including [3mShakespeareโs Words [0m(with Ben Crystal, 2002) and [3mโThink on My Wordsโ: Exploring Shakespeareโs 
Language [0m(2008); Frank Kermodeโs [3mShakespeareโs Language [0m(2001) is a more evocative and associative take on Shakespeareโs poetic 
use of rhetoric and vocabulary. 

[1mุดูุณุจูุฑ ูู ุงููุณุฑุญ [0m

Classic books on the Elizabethan theater are by Andrew Gurr again: [3mThe Shakespearean Stage, [0m(4th edn., 2009) and [3mPlaygoing in 
Shakespeareโs London [0m(3rd edn., 2004). Christie Carson and Farah Karim-Cooperโs [3mShakespeareโs Globe: A Theatrical Experiment [0m
(2008) is full of insights from a decade of productions in the rebuilt Globe on Londonโs Bankside. Tiffany Sternโs [3mMaking 
Shakespeare: From Stage to Page [0m(2004) understands the literary and theatrical contexts for Shakespeareโs work, and [3mher 
Shakespeare in Parts [0m(with Simon Palfrey, 2007) is a groundbreaking study of the way Shakespeareโs actors understood their roles. 
Martin Wigginsโs [3mShakespeare and the Drama of his Time [0m(2000) is recommended as a way to counter the myopia with which we often 
consider Shakespeare, and Arthur Kinneyโs [3mRenaissance Drama: An Anthology of Plays and Entertainments [0m(2nd edn., 2005), is the 
best place to sample contemporary writers. 

Cambridge University Pressโs series [3mPlayers of Shakespeare [0m(6 vols., 1985โ2004), supplemented by Michael Dobsonโs [3mPerforming 
Shakespeareโs Tragedies Today [0m(2006), provide a series of unique perspectives. Written by actors reflecting on their roles, these 
essays combine sophisticated analysis of individual actorsโ roles with a deep understanding of the play in which they perform. 
Carol Rutterโs [3mClamorous Voices: Shakespeareโs Women Today [0m(1988) gives Shakespeareโs female characters the same treatment: 
conversations between actors about their interpretation of, for example, [3mMeasure for Measureโs [0mIsabella or [3mAs You Like Itโs [0m
Rosalind, are revelatory about the sexual politics of specific productions at specific historical moments. Barbara Hodgdon, W.B. 
Worthen, Carol Rutter, and Bridget Escolme are all writers on Shakespeare in the theater who are methodologically sophisticated 
and genuinely revealing about performance: any of their works is well worth reading. 

[1mุชูุณูุฑ ุดูุณุจูุฑ [0m

There is no single way of interpreting Shakespeare: here we propose some recent survey volumes, all of which introduce a range of 
interpretative methods and frameworks and offer extensive suggestions in turn for further reading. Finally, we highlight some 
specific critical works to which we find ourselves returning for their acumen and provocation. 

There are any number of guides to Shakespeare: particularly useful are Robert Shaughnessyโs [3mThe Routledge Guide to William 
Shakespeare [0m(2011), which works through the plays and their historical, theatrical, and critical contexts; Stanley Wells and Lena 
Cowen Orlinโs [3mShakespeare: An Oxford Guide [0m(2003), which tries to set out, with detailed examples, different interpretative 
approaches; and [3mThe New Cambridge Companion to Shakespeare [0m(2011), edited by Margreta de Grazia and Stanley Wells, which covers 
different historical and critical aspects and has good suggestions for further reading. Russ McDonald collects significant 
twentieth-century criticism in his [3mShakespeare: An Anthology of Criticism and Theory, 1945โ2000 [0m(2003). There are two excellent 
series, the Oxford Shakespeare Topics (Oxford University Press) and Arden Critical Companions (Arden, Bloomsbury), giving 
up-to-date interventions in a range of topics, from biography to religion to literary theory. Works such as Dympna Callaghan 
(ed.), [3mA Feminist Companion to Shakespeare [0m(2000), Sonia Massai (ed.), [3mWorld-Wide Shakespeares: Local Appropriations in Film and 
Performance [0m(2005), and Ania Loomba and Martin Orkin (eds.), [3mPost-Colonial Shakespeares [0m(1998), give a sense of how the field has 
changed. We, and our students, love [3mDoing Shakespeare [0m, Simon Palfreyโs brilliant book of close reading (2nd edn., 2011); Marjorie 
Garberโs collection of provocative essays, [3mProfiling Shakespeare [0m(2008), is similarly lively. Michael Neillโs [3mPutting History to 
the Question: Power, Politics and Society in English Renaissance Drama [0m(2000) offers lucid, humanely historicist arguments. 

Gary Taylorโs [3mReinventing Shakespeare: A Cultural History from the Restoration to the Present [0m(1990; paperback 1991) reads like a 
critical version of Virginia Woolfโs novel [3mOrlando [0m, in which our hero morphs through centuries. Anything by Taylor is well worth 
reading: here he combines performance history, publication history, and political history; as an added bonus, each chapter is 
written in the style of the period it chronicles. Alexander Leggatt has published on every Shakespeare genre over thirty years: [3m
Shakespeareโs Comedy of Love [0m(1974, reprinted 2005), [3mShakespeareโs Political Drama [0m(1988), and [3mShakespeareโs Tragedies [0m(2005). His 
critical interpretations are based on the words in the play and the playโs theatrical effects: no other critic could get away with 
this limited focus, but Leggattโs critical insights show you why he can. A. D. Nuttallโs [3mShakespeare the Thinker [0m(2007) and Tony 
Tannerโs [3mPrefaces to Shakespeare [0m(2010) each offer a play-by-play approach, highly recommended if you require a refresher before 
going to the theater. Nuttall focuses on Shakespeareโs ideas; Tanner on the language in which those ideas are expressed. 

Our final injunction was to read Shakespeare himself: there is a plethora of available editions, each aimed at a particular 
readership. Although publishers offer Shakespeare series in individual volumes, itโs hard to recommend any one series uniformly: 
you will have your own criteria โ portability, price, font size, electronic or paper, amount of intrusive explanation, page design 
โ for choosing. We are drawn to different editions for different reasons: New Penguin for carrying to lectures, with their 
up-to-date and crisp introductions; Bedford St Martinโs โTexts and Contextsโ series for its inclusion of historical material to 
contextualize each play; Arden series 3 for extensive scholarship and annotation. The โShakespeare in Productionโ series from 
Cambridge University Press does not cover every play in the canon, but for those currently available in this series it gives a 
reading experience referenced to the myriad interpretations on stage: each line is keyed to how it has been interpreted by actors 
and directors, thus offering a quickly accessible range of interpretative possibilities. Elizabeth Schaferโs [3mThe Taming of the 
Shrew [0m(2002), for instance, is a particularly good volume to start with. You may wonder whether or why you would need to buy a new 
edition: is not a text from school or college days adequate? But interpretation of Shakespeare has developed, and new things are 
being discovered, as this book has shown: these changes and developments also affect the text we read. Publishers therefore are 
constantly updating and recommissioning editions to reflect this evolution. 

Three academic journals dominate the market for new work: [3mShakespeare Quarterly, [0mpublished by the Folger Shakespeare Library, [3m
Shakespeare Studies [0m(Farleigh Dickinson University Press), and [3mShakespeare Survey [0m(Cambridge University Press); [3mShakespeare [0m
(Routledge) is a relatively new entrant. The professional association in the USA is the Shakespeare Association of America: there 
are Shakespeare associations in India, Japan, Germany, Australia and New Zealand, Norway, Korea, Southern Africa, and many other 
countries. The British Shakespeare Association (http://www.britishshakespeare.ws/) has a wide base of teachers, theater 
practitioners, academics, and enthusiasts: the website highlights new work, Shakespeare in the news, and events and recordings.
